-- as
SELECT
-- date(ingestion_time) as date_, extract(hour from ingestion_time) as hour_, module
-- ,is_bad, config,
-- sum(timestamp_diff(end_time, start_time, SECOND)) as sum_cmd_time,
-- count(1)
-- , sum(if(data like '%syn-ack%',1,0)) as synack_count
-- asset_value,
*
FROM
`cyco-main.tinkles_data.raw_data`
WHERE 1=1
-- and DATE(ingestion_time) = "2021-08-05"
and ingestion_time > "2021-08-05 07:00:00" and ingestion_time < "2021-08-05 08:00:00"
and asset_value in ('52.213.141.246')
-- and config like '%-s%'
-- and config ='-Pn -sT -sV -p 80,443'
and module = 'openvas'
-- and ingestion_time >= "2021-07-01 00:00:00" and ingestion_time <= "2021-07-21 00:00:00"
-- group by 1,2,3,4,5

TLS</family><cvss_base>4.3</cvss_base><tags>cvss_base_vector=AV:N/AC:M/Au:N/C:P/I:N/A:N|summary=SSL/TLS connections are vulnerable to the 'BREACH' (Browser\n Reconnaissance &amp; Exfiltration via Adaptive Compression of Hypertext) attack.|insight=Angelo Prado, Neal Harris and Yoel Gluck reported that SSL/TLS\n attacks are still viable via a 'BREACH' (Browser Reconnaissance &amp; Exfiltration via Adaptive\n Compression of Hypertext) attack, which they describe as:\n\n While CRIME was mitigated by disabling TLS/SPDY compression (and by modifying gzip to allow for\n explicit separation of compression contexts in SPDY), BREACH attacks HTTP responses. These are\n compressed using the common HTTP compression, which is much more common than TLS-level\n compression. This allows essentially the same attack demonstrated by Duong and Rizzo, but without\n relying on TLS-level compression (as they anticipated).\n\n It is important to note that the attack is agnostic to the version of TLS/SSL, and does not\n require TLS-layer compression. Additionally, the attack works against any cipher suite. Against a\n stream cipher, the attack is simpler: The difference in sizes across response bodies is much more\n granular in this case. If a block cipher is used, additional work must be done to align the output\n to the cipher text blocks.|affected=BREACH is a category of vulnerabilities and not a specific\n instance affecting a specific piece of software. To be vulnerable, a web application must:\n\n - Be served from a server that uses HTTP-level compression\n\n - Reflect user-input in HTTP response bodies\n\n - Reflect a secret (such as a CSRF token) in HTTP response bodies|impact=The flaw makes it easier for man-in-the-middle attackers to\n obtain plaintext secret values.|solution=The following mitigation possibilities are available:\n\n 1. Disabling HTTP compression\n\n 2. Separating secrets from user input\n\n 3. Randomizing secrets per request\n\n 4. Masking secrets (effectively randomizing by XORing with a random secret per request)\n\n 5. Protecting vulnerable pages with CSRF\n\n 6. Length hiding (by adding random number of bytes to the responses)\n\n 7. Rate-limiting the requests\n\n Note: The mitigations are ordered by effectiveness (not by their practicality - as this may differ\n from one application to another).|vuldetect=Checks if the remote web server has HTTP compression enabled.\n\n Note: Even with HTTP compression enabled the web application hosted on the web server might not\n be vulnerable.
